<div class="thumbcaption">Flowchart of the life cycle of a Debian package</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Each software package has a <em>maintainer</em> that may be either one person or a team of Debian developers and non-developer maintainers.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup> The maintainer keeps track of <a href="/wiki/Upstream_(software_development)" title="Upstream (software development)">upstream</a> releases, and ensures that the package coheres with the rest of the distribution and meets the standards of quality of Debian. Packages may include modifications introduced by Debian to achieve compliance with Debian Policy, even to fix non-Debian specific bugs, although coordination with upstream developers is advised.<sup id="cite_ref-developer-duties_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-developer-duties-156"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The maintainer releases a new version by uploading the package to the "incoming" system, which verifies the integrity of the packages and their <a href="/wiki/Digital_signature" title="Digital signature">digital signatures</a>. If the package is found to be valid, it is installed in the package archive into an area called the "pool" and distributed every day to hundreds of <a href="/wiki/Web_mirror" title="Web mirror" class="mw-redirect">mirrors</a> worldwide. The upload must be signed using <a href="/wiki/OpenPGP" title="OpenPGP" class="mw-redirect">OpenPGP</a>-compatible software.<sup id="cite_ref-distributions_46-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-distributions-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> All Debian developers have individual <a href="/wiki/Public-key_cryptography" title="Public-key cryptography">cryptographic key pairs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159"><span>[</span>159<span>]</span></a></sup> Developers are responsible for any package they upload even if the packaging was prepared by another contributor.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160"><span>[</span>160<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Initially, an accepted package is only available in the <i>Unstable</i> branch.<sup id="cite_ref-distributions_46-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-distributions-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> For a package to become a candidate for the next release, it must migrate to the <i>Testing</i> branch by meeting the following:<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161"><span>[</span>161<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>It has been in <i>Unstable</i> for a certain length of time that depends on the urgency of the changes.</li>
<li>It does not have "release-critical" bugs, except for the ones already present in <i>Testing</i>. Release-critical bugs are those considered serious enough that they make the package unsuitable for release.</li>
<li>There are no outdated versions in <i>Unstable</i> for any release ports.</li>
<li>The migration does not break any packages in <i>Testing</i>.</li>
<li>Its dependencies can be satisfied by packages already in <i>Testing</i> or by packages being migrated at the same time.</li>
<li>The migration is not blocked by a freeze.</li>
</ul>
<p>Thus, a release-critical bug in a new version of a shared library on which many packages depend may prevent those packages from entering <i>Testing</i>, because the updated library must meet the requirements too.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162"><span>[</span>162<span>]</span></a></sup> From the branch viewpoint, the migration process happens twice per day, rendering <i>Testing</i> in <a href="/wiki/Perpetual_beta" title="Perpetual beta">perpetual beta</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-distributions_46-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-distributions-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Periodically, the release team publishes guidelines to the developers in order to ready the release. A new release occurs after a freeze, when all important software is reasonably up-to-date in the <i>Testing</i> branch and any other significant issues are solved. At that time, all packages in the <i>Testing</i> branch become the new <i>Stable</i> branch.<sup id="cite_ref-distributions_46-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-distributions-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> Although freeze dates are time-based,<sup id="cite_ref-two-year-cycle_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-two-year-cycle-127"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> release dates are not, which are announced by the release managers a couple of weeks beforehand.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163"><span>[</span>163<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A version of a package can belong to more than one branch, usually <i>Testing</i> and <i>Unstable</i>. It is possible for a package to keep the same version between stable releases and be part of <i>Oldstable</i>, <i>Stable</i>, <i>Testing</i> and <i>Unstable</i> at the same time.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup> Each branch can be seen as a collection of pointers into the package "pool" mentioned above.<sup id="cite_ref-distributions_46-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-distributions-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Security">Security</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Debian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Security">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Debian project handles security through <a href="/wiki/Full_disclosure_(computer_security)" title="Full disclosure (computer security)">public disclosure</a> rather than through <a href="/wiki/Security_through_obscurity" title="Security through obscurity">obscurity</a>. Debian security advisories are compatible with the <a href="/wiki/Common_Vulnerabilities_and_Exposures" title="Common Vulnerabilities and Exposures">Common Vulnerabilities and Exposures</a> dictionary, are usually coordinated with other free software vendors and are published the same day a vulnerability is made public.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165"><span>[</span>165<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166"><span>[</span>166<span>]</span></a></sup> There used to be a security audit project that focused on packages in the stable release looking for security bugs;<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167"><span>[</span>167<span>]</span></a></sup> Steve Kemp, who started the project, retired in 2011 but resumed his activities and applied to rejoin in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168"><span>[</span>168<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <i>Stable</i> branch is supported by the Debian security team; <i>Oldstable</i> is supported for one year.<sup id="cite_ref-securityfaq_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-securityfaq-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> Although Squeeze is not officially supported, Debian is coordinating an effort to provide <a href="/wiki/Long-term_support" title="Long-term support">long-term support</a> until February 2016, five years after the initial release, but only for the IA-32 and x86-64 platforms.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Testing</i> is supported by the <i>Testing</i> security team, but does not receive updates in as timely a manner as <i>Stable</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171"><span>[</span>171<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Unstable</i> '​s security is left for the package maintainers.<sup id="cite_ref-securityfaq_49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-securityfaq-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Debian project offers documentation and tools to <a href="/wiki/Hardening_(computing)" title="Hardening (computing)">harden</a> a Debian installation both manually and automatically.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172"><span>[</span>172<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Security-Enhanced_Linux" title="Security-Enhanced Linux">Security-Enhanced Linux</a> and <a href="/wiki/AppArmor" title="AppArmor">AppArmor</a> support is available but disabled by default.<sup id="cite_ref-new-in-lenny-arm_103-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-new-in-lenny-arm-103"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup> Debian provides an optional hardening wrapper, and does not harden all of its software by default using <a href="/wiki/GNU_Compiler_Collection" title="GNU Compiler Collection">gcc</a> features such as <a href="/wiki/Position-independent_code" title="Position-independent code">PIE</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buffer_overflow_protection" title="Buffer overflow protection">buffer overflow protection</a>, unlike operating systems such as <a href="/wiki/OpenBSD" title="OpenBSD">OpenBSD</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173"><span>[</span>173<span>]</span></a></sup> but tries to build as many packages as possible with hardening flags.<sup id="cite_ref-new-in-7_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-new-in-7-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2008_OpenSSL_vulnerability">2008 OpenSSL vulnerability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Debian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: 2008 OpenSSL vulnerability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In May 2008, it was revealed that a Debian developer discovered that the <a href="/wiki/OpenSSL" title="OpenSSL">OpenSSL</a> package distributed with Debian and derivatives such as <a href="/wiki/Ubuntu_(operating_system)" title="Ubuntu (operating system)">Ubuntu</a>, made a variety of security keys vulnerable to a <a href="/wiki/Random_number_generator_attack" title="Random number generator attack">random number generator attack</a>, since only 32,767 different keys were generated.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174"><span>[</span>174<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175"><span>[</span>175<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-garfinkel_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-garfinkel-176"><span>[</span>176<span>]</span></a></sup> The security weakness was caused by changes made in 2006 by another Debian developer in response to memory debugger warnings.<sup id="cite_ref-garfinkel_176-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-garfinkel-176"><span>[</span>176<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177"><span>[</span>177<span>]</span></a></sup> The complete resolution procedure was cumbersome because patching the security hole was not enough; it involved regenerating all affected keys and certificates.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178"><span>[</span>178<span>]</span></a></sup> Being introduced by Debian, the vulnerability caused anger and embarrassment among Debian developers.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179"><span>[</span>179<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180"><span>[</span>180<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Derivatives">Derivatives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Debian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Derivatives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Debian is one of the most popular Linux distributions, and many <a href="/wiki/Debian_family" title="Debian family" class="mw-redirect">other distributions</a> have been created from the Debian codebase, including Ubuntu and <a href="/wiki/Knoppix" title="Knoppix">Knoppix</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181"><span>[</span>181<span>]</span></a></sup> As of 2014<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Debian&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/DistroWatch" title="DistroWatch">DistroWatch</a> lists 135 active Debian derivatives.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182"><span>[</span>182<span>]</span></a></sup> The Debian project provides its derivatives with guidelines for best practices and encourages derivatives to merge their work back into Debian.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183"><span>[</span>183<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHertzog2013429_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHertzog2013429-184"><span>[</span>184<span>]</span></a></sup> A sign of cooperation with Ubuntu can be seen in the Debian package tracker.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185"><span>[</span>185<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Debian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Debian_releases" title="List of Debian releases">List of Debian releases</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birth_.281993.E2.80.931998.29">Birth (1993–1998)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Debian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Birth (1993–1998)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Debian was first announced on August 16, 1993, by <a href="/wiki/Ian_Murdock" title="Ian Murdock">Ian Murdock</a>, who initially called the system "the Debian Linux Release".<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186"><span>[</span>186<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187"><span>[</span>187<span>]</span></a></sup> The word "Debian" was formed as a <a href="/wiki/Portmanteau" title="Portmanteau">combination</a> of the first name of his then-girlfriend Debra Lynn and his own first name.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188"><span>[</span>188<span>]</span></a></sup> Prior to Debian's release, the <a href="/wiki/Softlanding_Linux_System" title="Softlanding Linux System">Softlanding Linux System</a> (SLS) had been a popular Linux distribution and the basis for <a href="/wiki/Slackware" title="Slackware">Slackware</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-grassroots_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grassroots-189"><span>[</span>189<span>]</span></a></sup> The perceived poor maintenance and prevalence of <a href="/wiki/Software_bug" title="Software bug">bugs</a> in SLS motivated Murdock to launch a new distribution.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScheetz199817_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScheetz199817-190"><span>[</span>190<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Debian 0.01, released on September 15, 1993, was the first of several internal releases.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191"><span>[</span>191<span>]</span></a></sup> Version 0.91 was virtually the first public release,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScheetz199818_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScheetz199818-192"><span>[</span>192<span>]</span></a></sup> providing support through mailing lists hosted at Pixar.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193"><span>[</span>193<span>]</span></a></sup> The release included the Debian Linux Manifesto, outlining Murdock's view for the new <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">operating system</a>. In it he called for the creation of a distribution to be maintained openly, in the spirit of Linux and GNU.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194"><span>[</span>194<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Debian project released the 0.9x versions in 1994 and 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-releases_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-releases-195"><span>[</span>195<span>]</span></a></sup> During this time it was sponsored by the <a href="/wiki/Free_Software_Foundation" title="Free Software Foundation">Free Software Foundation</a> for one year.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196"><span>[</span>196<span>]</span></a></sup> Ian Murdock delegated the base system, the core packages of Debian, to Bruce Perens and Murdock focused on the management of the growing project.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScheetz199818_192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScheetz199818-192"><span>[</span>192<span>]</span></a></sup> The first ports to non-<a href="/wiki/IA-32" title="IA-32">IA-32</a> architectures began in 1995, and Debian 1.1 was released in 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-history4_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history4-197"><span>[</span>197<span>]</span></a></sup> By that time and thanks to <a href="/wiki/Ian_Jackson" title="Ian Jackson">Ian Jackson</a>, the dpkg package manager was already an essential part of Debian.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKrafft200531.E2.80.9332_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKrafft200531.E2.80.9332-198"><span>[</span>198<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1996, Bruce Perens assumed the project leadership. Perens was a controversial leader, regarded as authoritarian and strongly attached to Debian.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHertzog20139_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHertzog20139-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> He drafted a <a href="/wiki/Social_contract" title="Social contract">social contract</a> and edited suggestions from a month-long discussion into the Debian Social Contract and the Debian Free Software Guidelines.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199"><span>[</span>199<span>]</span></a></sup> After the FSF withdrew their sponsorship in the midst of the <a href="/wiki/Free_and_open-source_software" title="Free and open-source software">free software vs. open source debate</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200"><span>[</span>200<span>]</span></a></sup> Perens initiated the creation of the legal <a href="/wiki/Umbrella_organization" title="Umbrella organization">umbrella organization</a> <a href="/wiki/Software_in_the_Public_Interest" title="Software in the Public Interest">Software in the Public Interest</a> instead of seeking renewed involvement with the FSF.<sup id="cite_ref-history4_197-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history4-197"><span>[</span>197<span>]</span></a></sup> He led the conversion of the project from <a href="/wiki/A.out" title="A.out">a.out</a> to <a href="/wiki/Executable_and_Linkable_Format" title="Executable and Linkable Format">ELF</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScheetz199819_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScheetz199819-201"><span>[</span>201<span>]</span></a></sup> He created the <a href="/wiki/BusyBox" title="BusyBox">BusyBox</a> program to make it possible to run a Debian installer on a single floppy, and wrote a new installer.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202"><span>[</span>202<span>]</span></a></sup> By the time Debian 1.2 was released, the project had grown to nearly two hundred volunteers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScheetz199819_201-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScheetz199819-201"><span>[</span>201<span>]</span></a></sup> Perens left the project in 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203"><span>[</span>203<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Ian Jackson became the leader in 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204"><span>[</span>204<span>]</span></a></sup> Debian 2.0 introduced the second official port, <a href="/wiki/Motorola_68000_family" title="Motorola 68000 family" class="mw-redirect">m68k</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-releases_195-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-releases-195"><span>[</span>195<span>]</span></a></sup> During this time the first port to a non-Linux kernel, <a href="/wiki/Debian_GNU/Hurd" title="Debian GNU/Hurd">Debian GNU/Hurd</a>, was started.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205"><span>[</span>205<span>]</span></a></sup> On December 2, the first Debian Constitution was ratified.<sup id="cite_ref-constitution_128-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-constitution-128"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leader_election_.281999.E2.80.932005.29">Leader election (1999–2005)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Debian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Leader election (1999–2005)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>From 1999, the project leader was elected yearly.<sup id="cite_ref-vote-info_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vote-info-138"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Packaging_Tool" title="Advanced Packaging Tool">Advanced Packaging Tool</a> was deployed with Debian 2.1.<sup id="cite_ref-releases_195-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-releases-195"><span>[</span>195<span>]</span></a></sup> The amount of applicants was overwhelming and the project established the new member process.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEColeman2013141_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEColeman2013141-206"><span>[</span>206<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207"><span>[</span>207<span>]</span></a></sup> The first Debian derivatives, namely <a href="/wiki/Libranet" title="Libranet">Libranet</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208"><span>[</span>208<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Corel_Linux" title="Corel Linux">Corel Linux</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stormix" title="Stormix">Stormix</a>'s Storm Linux, were started in 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-history4_197-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history4-197"><span>[</span>197<span>]</span></a></sup> The 2.2 release in 2000 was dedicated to Joel Klecker, a developer who died of <a href="/wiki/Duchenne_muscular_dystrophy" title="Duchenne muscular dystrophy">Duchenne muscular dystrophy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209"><span>[</span>209<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In late 2000, the project reorganized the archive with new package "pools" and created the <i>Testing</i> distribution, made up of packages considered stable, to reduce the freeze for the next release.<sup id="cite_ref-history4_197-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history4-197"><span>[</span>197<span>]</span></a></sup> In the same year, developers began holding an annual conference called <a href="/wiki/DebConf" title="DebConf">DebConf</a> with talks and workshops for developers and technical users.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210"><span>[</span>210<span>]</span></a></sup> In May 2001, <a href="/wiki/Hewlett-Packard" title="Hewlett-Packard">Hewlett-Packard</a> announced plans to base its Linux development on Debian.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211"><span>[</span>211<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In July 2002, the project released version 3.0, code-named Woody, the first release to include cryptographic software, a free licensed KDE and <a href="/wiki/Internationalization_and_localization" title="Internationalization and localization">internationalization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKrafft200533_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKrafft200533-212"><span>[</span>212<span>]</span></a></sup> During these last release cycles, the Debian project drew considerable criticism from the free software community because of the long time between stable releases.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213"><span>[</span>213<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214"><span>[</span>214<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215"><span>[</span>215<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Some events disturbed the project while working on Sarge, as Debian servers were attacked by fire and hackers.<sup id="cite_ref-history4_197-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history4-197"><span>[</span>197<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216"><span>[</span>216<span>]</span></a></sup> One of the most memorable was the Vancouver prospectus.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEColeman2013150_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEColeman2013150-217"><span>[</span>217<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218"><span>[</span>218<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219"><span>[</span>219<span>]</span></a></sup> After a meeting held in <a href="/wiki/Vancouver" title="Vancouver">Vancouver</a>, release manager Steve Langasek announced a plan to reduce the number of supported ports to four in order to shorten future release cycles.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220"><span>[</span>220<span>]</span></a></sup> There was a large reaction because the proposal looked more like a decision and because such a drop would damage Debian's aim to be "the universal operating system".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEColeman2013153.E2.80.93154_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEColeman2013153.E2.80.93154-221"><span>[</span>221<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222"><span>[</span>222<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223"><span>[</span>223<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sarge_and_later_releases_.282005.E2.80.932015.29">Sarge and later releases (2005–2015)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Debian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Sarge and later releases (2005–2015)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Debian_Etch-ja.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Debian_Etch-ja.png/250px-Debian_Etch-ja.png" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Debian_Etch-ja.png/375px-Debian_Etch-ja.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Debian_Etch-ja.png/500px-Debian_Etch-ja.png 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Debian_Etch-ja.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Debian 4.0 Etch (2007)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The 3.1 Sarge release was made in June 2005. This release updated 73% of the software and included over 9,000 new packages. A new installer with a modular design, <a href="/wiki/Debian-Installer" title="Debian-Installer">Debian-Installer</a>, allowed installations with <a href="/wiki/RAID" title="RAID">RAID</a>, <a href="/wiki/XFS" title="XFS">XFS</a> and <a href="/wiki/Logical_Volume_Manager_(Linux)" title="Logical Volume Manager (Linux)">LVM</a> support, improved hardware detection, made installations easier for novice users, and was translated into almost forty languages. An installation manual and release notes were in ten and fifteen languages respectively. The efforts of <a href="/wiki/Skolelinux" title="Skolelinux">Skolelinux</a>, <a href="/wiki/Debian-Med" title="Debian-Med">Debian-Med</a> and Debian-Accessibility raised the number of packages that were educational, had a medical affiliation, and ones made for people with disabilities.<sup id="cite_ref-history4_197-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history4-197"><span>[</span>197<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sargenew_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sargenew-224"><span>[</span>224<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:66px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Iceweasel_icon.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Iceweasel_icon.svg/64px-Iceweasel_icon.svg.png" width="64" height="64" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Iceweasel_icon.svg/96px-Iceweasel_icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Iceweasel_icon.svg/128px-Iceweasel_icon.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="128" data-file-height="128" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Iceweasel_icon.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Iceweasel" title="Iceweasel" class="mw-redirect">Iceweasel</a> logo</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2006, as a result of a much-publicized dispute, <a href="/wiki/Mozilla_Corporation_software_rebranded_by_the_Debian_project" title="Mozilla Corporation software rebranded by the Debian project">Mozilla software was rebranded in Debian</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Firefox" title="Firefox">Firefox</a> becoming Iceweasel and <a href="/wiki/Mozilla_Thunderbird" title="Mozilla Thunderbird">Thunderbird</a> becoming Icedove. The Mozilla Corporation stated that software with unapproved modifications could not be distributed under the Firefox trademark. Two reasons that Debian modifies the Firefox software are to change the non-free artwork and to provide security patches.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225"><span>[</span>225<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226"><span>[</span>226<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A fund-raising experiment, Dunc-Tank, was created to solve the release cycle problem and release managers were paid to work full-time;<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227"><span>[</span>227<span>]</span></a></sup> in response, unpaid developers slowed down their work and the release was delayed.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228"><span>[</span>228<span>]</span></a></sup> Debian 4.0 (Etch) was released in April 2007, featuring the x86-64 port and a graphical installer.<sup id="cite_ref-releases_195-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-releases-195"><span>[</span>195<span>]</span></a></sup> Debian 5.0 (Lenny) was released in February 2009, supporting Marvell's <a href="/wiki/Orion_(system-on-a-chip)" title="Orion (system-on-a-chip)">Orion</a> platform and netbooks such as the <a href="/wiki/Asus_Eee_PC" title="Asus Eee PC">Asus Eee PC</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-lenny_released_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lenny_released-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> The release was dedicated to Thiemo Seufer, a developer who died in a car accident.<sup id="cite_ref-thiemo_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thiemo-229"><span>[</span>229<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Debian_6.0.2.1.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Debian_6.0.2.1.png/250px-Debian_6.0.2.1.png" width="250" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Debian_6.0.2.1.png/375px-Debian_6.0.2.1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Debian_6.0.2.1.png/500px-Debian_6.0.2.1.png 2x" data-file-width="1366" data-file-height="768" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Debian_6.0.2.1.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Debian 6.0 Squeeze (2011)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In July 2009, the policy of time-based development freezes on a two-year cycle was announced. Time-based freezes are intended to blend the predictability of time based releases with Debian's policy of feature based releases, and to reduce overall freeze time.<sup id="cite_ref-two-year-cycle_127-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-two-year-cycle-127"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> The Squeeze cycle was going to be especially short; however, this initial schedule was abandoned.<sup id="cite_ref-two-year-cycle-2_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-two-year-cycle-2-230"><span>[</span>230<span>]</span></a></sup> In September 2010, the <a href="/wiki/Backporting" title="Backporting">backports</a> service became official, providing more recent versions of some software for the stable release.<sup id="cite_ref-news-backports-official_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news-backports-official-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Debian 6.0 (Squeeze) was released in February 2011, introduced Debian GNU/kFreeBSD as a technology preview, featured a dependency-based boot system, and moved problematic firmware to the non-free area.<sup id="cite_ref-squeeze_released_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-squeeze_released-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> Debian 7.0 (Wheezy) was released in May 2013, featuring multiarch support<sup id="cite_ref-news-wheezy-released_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news-wheezy-released-231"><span>[</span>231<span>]</span></a></sup> and Debian 8.0 (Jessie) was released in April 2015, using <a href="/wiki/Systemd" title="Systemd">systemd</a> as the new init system.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232"><span>[</span>232<span>]</span></a></sup> At present<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Debian&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Debian is still in development and new packages are uploaded to <i>Unstable</i> every day.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233"><span>[</span>233<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Throughout Debian's lifetime, both the Debian distribution and its website have won various awards from different organizations,<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234"><span>[</span>234<span>]</span></a></sup> including <i>Server Distribution of the Year</i> 2011,<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235"><span>[</span>235<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>The best Linux distro of 2011</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236"><span>[</span>236<span>]</span></a></sup> and a <i>Best of the Net</i> award for October 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237"><span>[</span>237<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Debian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
